from where will my help come?

**LXX (Greek):** κύριος φυλάσσω ὁ εἴσοδος σύ καί ὁ ἔξοδος σύ ἀπό ὁ νῦν καί ἕως ὁ αἰών

### INTRODUCTION ESSAY (for review)
# Introduction Essay

The fifteen Songs of Ascents (Psalms 120–134) constitute a distinct liturgical collection, and among them, Psalm 121 occupies a unique position. At first glance, it appears deceptively simple—a traveler's prayer for protection on the journey to Jerusalem. But the psalm's true sophistication lies not in complexity but in compression: it transforms individual anxiety into communal assurance through a dramatic liturgical dialogue that systematically dismantles Ancient Near Eastern religious anxieties and replaces them with the all-encompassing protection of YHWH as cosmic creator.

The psalm's opening question—אֶשָּׂא עֵינַי אֶל־הֶהָרִים מֵאַיִן יָבֹא עֶזְרִי ("I lift my eyes to the mountains; from where will my help come?")—is deliberately ambiguous. The mountains (*he-harim*) are not simply geographical features of the pilgrimage landscape. In the Ancient Near East, mountains were the dwelling places of gods, the high places where the divine and human realms met. The Canaanite storm god Baal resided on Mount Zaphon; the Mesopotamian gods convened on the cosmic mountain. Are these mountains a source of threat—dangerous terrain harboring bandits and wild beasts, or pagan cult sites atop Israel's hills? Or are they a source of hope—Mount Zion itself, the dwelling place of YHWH? The ambiguity is strategic. It represents the pilgrim's moment of vulnerability and uncertainty, standing at the threshold of the journey with eyes scanning the horizon for help that might not come.

The answer arrives swiftly and decisively: עֶזְרִי מֵעִם יְהֹוָה עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ ("My help comes from YHWH, maker of heaven and earth"). This creedal formula, which appears elsewhere in the Psalter (Ps 115:15, 124:8, 134:3) and biblical literature (Gen 14:19, 22), is not mere theological decoration. It is a cosmic sovereignty claim that moves the psalm from the particular (mountains) to the universal (all creation). The epithet "maker of heaven and earth" (*oseh shamayim va-aretz*) functions polemically: it asserts that the source of protection is not any localized deity of mountain or storm, but the God who made the entire cosmos—mountains, heavens, and all. As Radak notes, this formula emphasizes that "הכל בידו לעשות" ("everything is in His power to do"). The pilgrim's help comes not from any geographical or cultic location, but from the one whose creative power encompasses all locations.

At this pivotal moment, the psalm executes its most striking rhetorical move: a dramatic shift from first-person confession (vv. 1–2) to second-person priestly assurance (vv. 3–8). This is not a stylistic accident but a liturgical structure. The scholarly consensus, reflected in commentaries from medieval exegetes through modern scholarship, understands this as a dialogue between pilgrim and priest (or temple attendant). The pilgrim voices the question; the priest responds with blessing. As Metzudat David explains: "כאלו אחד יאמר לחבירו דרך נחמה הנה עזרה טובה היא" ("It is as if one says to another, by way of comfort, 'Behold, help is good'"). This dialogical structure transforms the psalm from private prayer into communal liturgy, from individual anxiety into corporate reassurance.

The priestly response builds systematically, centered on the six-fold repetition of the root שמר (*shamar*, "to guard/keep"). This incantatory repetition—appearing in verses 3 (twice), 4, 5, 7 (twice), and 8—is the psalm's sonic and thematic heartbeat. The verb *shamar* carries rich covenantal resonance throughout the Hebrew Bible, denoting not passive watching but active, covenant-faithful protection. When Genesis 2:15 says that humans were placed in Eden "to work it and *guard it*" (*le-ovdah u-le-shomrah*), or when Deuteronomy repeatedly commands Israel to "*guard* the commandments" (*shamor et ha-mitzvot*), the term connotes vigilant care and faithful preservation. Here, that same covenantal vigilance is applied to God's protection of the pilgrim.

The psalm's polemical thrust becomes most explicit in verses 3–4, which employ emphatic negation to contrast YHWH with other deities. The double negative—לֹא־יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן ("neither slumbers nor sleeps")—likely targets Ancient Near Eastern myths of divine inattention. The Ugaritic Baal cycle depicts the storm god's seasonal death and slumber; the prophet Elijah mockingly suggests that Baal might be sleeping and need awakening (1 Kgs 18:27: "קְרָא בְקוֹל־גָּדוֹל כִּי־אֱלֹהִים הוּא... אוּלַי יָשֵׁן הוּא וְיִקָץ"—"Cry with a loud voice, for he is a god... perhaps he is asleep and will awaken"). Against this backdrop, the assertion that YHWH "neither slumbers nor sleeps" is not poetic flourish but theological polemic. As the Meiri observes, "התנומה היא שינה קלה והשינה שינה גמורה" ("slumber is light sleep and sleep is deep sleep")—the doubling intensifies the claim of absolute, uninterrupted vigilance.

The expansion from individual to communal in verse 4—שֹׁמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל ("guardian of Israel")—is theologically crucial. The personal protection promised to the individual pilgrim is grounded in God's covenantal protection of the entire people. This movement from singular to collective and back to singular creates a dynamic interplay between personal and communal identity, suggesting that individual trust is not isolated piety but participation in Israel's corporate relationship with God.

Verses 5–6 deploy a cluster of protective metaphors that address both literal and symbolic dangers. The image of YHWH as "shade" (*tzel*) operates on multiple registers. Literally, shade is essential protection from the sun's deadly heat in the ancient Near Eastern climate—sunstroke (*shemesh lo yakkekah*, "the sun will not strike you") was a genuine threat to travelers. The psalm extends this protection to nighttime, noting that "the moon by night" (*ve-yareach ba-laylah*) will not harm. While lunar danger might seem puzzling to modern readers, ancient beliefs about lunar influence on mental health (reflected in terms like "lunacy") and nocturnal hazards make this a comprehensive day-night protection formula—a merismus encompassing all time.

The metaphor of shade also carries theological depth. As Ibn Ezra notes, the phrase יְהֹוָה צִלְּךָ עַל־יַד יְמִינֶךָ ("YHWH is your shade at your right hand") suggests military protection—the right hand being the weapon side, vulnerable in combat. The divine warrior stations himself as shield at the fighter's exposed flank. This imagery of God as protective covering recurs throughout the Psalter, most famously in Psalm 91:1: "יֹשֵׁב בְּסֵתֶר עֶלְיוֹן בְּצֵל שַׁדַּי יִתְלוֹנָן" ("Whoever dwells in the shelter of the Most High will lodge in the shadow of the Almighty").

The psalm's climactic verses (7–8) achieve comprehensive totality through multiple merismus pairs. The phrase מִכָּל־רָע ("from all evil") encompasses every possible harm—physical, spiritual, cosmic. The specification "He will guard your *nefesh*" (soul/life) moves from external threats to the essential self. The final verse deploys perhaps the most significant merismus in biblical literature: צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ ("your going out and your coming in"). This phrase carries technical meanings across biblical Hebrew. It can denote military campaigns (Num 27:17: "אֲשֶׁר יֵצֵא לִפְנֵיהֶם וַאֲשֶׁר יָבֹא לִפְנֵיהֶם"—"who shall go out before them and who shall come in before them"), but more fundamentally, it is a merismus for the totality of life's activities. Deuteronomy's blessings and curses employ this formula repeatedly: "בָּרוּךְ אַתָּה בְּבֹאֶךָ וּבָרוּךְ אַתָּה בְּצֵאתֶךָ" ("Blessed shall you be in your coming in, and blessed shall you be in your going out," Deut 28:6).

The temporal pair מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם ("from now and forever") completes the totality. Spatial (going/coming), temporal (now/forever), and comprehensive (all evil)—the psalm has constructed a rhetorical fortress of divine protection that leaves no gap, no moment, no threat unguarded. This is not hyperbole but theological assertion: the God who made all cannot be limited by any of the boundaries that constrain lesser powers.

Why did this psalm earn its place in the biblical canon and achieve such prominence in Jewish liturgical life? Its power lies in the perfect marriage of form and function. The six-fold *shamar* repetition does not merely describe protection—it enacts it. The liturgy transforms speech into reality; the very recitation becomes an invocation of the protection it proclaims. The psalm addresses universal human anxiety—the vulnerability of the journey, the threat of the unknown—but grounds that anxiety in Israel's specific covenantal relationship with YHWH. It speaks both to the particular historical context of pilgrimage to Jerusalem and to the timeless human condition of seeking security in an uncertain world.

The psalm's dialogical structure may also hint at its origins in temple liturgy. Scholars have long noted that the Songs of Ascents share distinctive linguistic features, including "staircase parallelism" (anadiplosis) where key words climb from line to line. While Psalm 121 doesn't employ this device as extensively as some of its companions, its movement from question to answer, from individual to priest, suggests a liturgical performance where roles were clearly delineated and the exchange itself was part of the ritual. The pilgrim ascending toward Jerusalem would have experienced this psalm not as solitary meditation but as communal liturgy, with temple personnel responding to the pilgrim's voiced anxiety with authoritative assurance.

In the end, Psalm 121 achieves theological sophistication through structural simplicity. It needs no complex imagery, no elaborate metaphors, no extended narratives. Its power lies in the relentless accumulation of divine protection, the systematic dismantling of every source of anxiety, and the transformation of individual fear into communal trust. The pilgrim who began by looking to the mountains—whether in hope or fear—ends by looking beyond all mountains to the God who made them, who neither sleeps nor slumbers, who guards with perfect vigilance from this moment until the end of time.

# Modern Jewish Liturgical Use

Psalm 121 appears extensively in Jewish liturgy, functioning primarily as a prayer for divine protection and reassurance. The psalm is recited in its entirety as a supplementary text following the silent Amidah in weekday Maariv and Shabbat Mincha across Ashkenaz, Sefard, and Edot HaMizrach traditions, serving as a concluding prayer of comfort after the main statutory service. It also appears in the Rosh Hashanah liturgy—after Maariv at the holiday's conclusion and within an expanded Pesukei Dezimra in the Edot HaMizrach morning service. Additionally, the psalm is incorporated into the monthly Kiddush Levanah (Sanctification of the Moon) ritual.

Individual verses and phrases from Psalm 121 have been extracted for specific liturgical contexts. The verse עֶזְרִי מֵעִם יְהֹוָה עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ ("My help is from YHWH, Maker of heaven and earth") functions as a congregational response in the Ashkenazic Kaddish Shalem, recited immediately after the Aramaic plea: "יְהֵא שְׁלָמָא רַבָּא מִן שְׁמַיָא וְחַיִּים עָלֵינוּ... וְאִמְרוּ אָמֵן: עֶזְרִי מֵעִם יְהוָה עשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ" ("May there be abundant peace from heaven and life upon us... and say Amen: My help is from YHWH, Maker of heaven and earth"). This transforms the psalm's confession of trust into a liturgical affirmation following prayer.

The verse הִנֵּה לֹא־יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן שׁוֹמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל ("Behold, the Guardian of Israel neither slumbers nor sleeps") appears in multiple protective contexts: within the bedtime Shema (K'riat Shema Al HaMitah) after the Priestly Blessing—"יִשָּׂא יְהֹוָה פָּנָיו אֵלֶֽיךָ וְיָשֵׂם לְךָ שָׁלוֹם: הִנֵּה לֺא יָנוּם וְלֺא יִישָׁן שׁוֹמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל" ("May YHWH lift His face toward you and grant you peace: Behold, the Guardian of Israel neither slumbers nor sleeps")—and embedded in the Yom Kippur Vidui. The closing verse יְהֹוָה יִשְׁמׇר־צֵאתְךָ֥ וּבוֹאֶ֑ךָ appears in the Shabbat home ritual (particularly Sefardic) following Shalom Aleichem, paired with Psalm 91:11 to create a blessing of angelic and divine protection at the threshold: "כִּ֣י מַ֭לְאָכָיו יְצַוֶּה־לָּ֑ךְ לִ֝שְׁמׇרְךָ֗ בְּכׇל־דְּרָכֶֽיךָ: יְֽהֹוָ֗ה יִשְׁמׇר־צֵאתְךָ֥ וּבוֹאֶ֑ךָ" ("For He will command His angels concerning you, to guard you in all your ways: YHWH will guard your going out and your coming in").

This liturgical distribution reveals how Jewish tradition has understood Psalm 121: as a comprehensive prayer of protection applicable to daily vulnerability (bedtime), weekly transition (Shabbat), and life's journeys (travel prayers). The extraction of individual verses for insertion into other prayers demonstrates the tradition's recognition that the psalm's protective assurances retain their power even when detached from their original poetic context, functioning as portable theological affirmations of divine guardianship.

### VERSE-BY-VERSE COMMENTARY (for review)
# Verse-by-Verse Commentary: Psalm 121

## Verse 1

**שִׁיר לַמַּעֲלוֹת; אֶשָּׂא עֵינַי אֶל־הֶהָרִים, מֵאַיִן יָבֹא עֶזְרִי.**

The psalm opens with a dramatic *mise-en-scène*: a pilgrim, standing at some indeterminate distance from Jerusalem, lifts his eyes toward the mountains and asks an urgent question: "From where will my help come?" (מֵאַיִן יָבֹא עֶזְרִי). The superscription *shir lama'alot* (שִׁיר לַמַּעֲלוֹת, "A song for ascents") situates this within the collection of Psalms 120–134, traditionally associated with pilgrimage to Jerusalem for the three festivals (Deuteronomy 16:16 speaks of עֲלִיָּה לָרֶגֶל, "going up for the festival"). Yet this opening verse is deliberately ambiguous—a hermeneutic puzzle that has fascinated commentators from the medieval period forward.

The crux is the phrase *el he-harim* (אֶל־הֶהָרִים, "to/toward the mountains"). The preposition אֶל (el) means "to" or "toward," not "from," suggesting the mountains are the *object* of the lifting gaze, not necessarily its *source* of help. Are these mountains threatening (dangerous terrain harboring bandits, pagan high places like those condemned in Deuteronomy 12:2: "עַל הֶהָרִים הָרָמִים וְעַל הַגְּבָעוֹת וְתַחַת כָּל־עֵץ רַעֲנָן—upon the high mountains and upon the hills and under every leafy tree")? Or are they promising—Mount Zion itself, the "holy mountain" of God (הַר־קָדְשֶׁךָ, Psalm 15:1)?

Radak's commentary captures this tension perfectly: "כְּמוֹ הַמְצַפֶּה עֵזֶר מֵרָחוֹק שֶׁעוֹלֶה עַל הֶהָרִים וְנוֹשֵׂא עֵינָיו אֵילָךְ וְאֵילָךְ אִם יָבֹאוּ לִי עוֹזְרִים—Like one who looks for help from afar, who ascends the mountains and lifts his eyes this way and that, [asking] if help will come to me." The pilgrim's question is genuine, not rhetorical. The mountains, in ancient Near Eastern cosmology, were the dwelling places of gods (compare Ugaritic *Ṣapānu*, the mountain of Baal). The psalmist stands at a threshold of uncertainty: Will help come from these mountains—from their pagan deities, from their treacherous passes, from Zion's God?

The verb *essa* (אֶשָּׂא, "I lift up") appears in identical construction in Psalm 123:1: "אֵלֶיךָ נָשָׂאתִי אֶת־עֵינַי הַיּשְׁבִי בַּשָּׁמָיִם—To You I lift up my eyes, O You who are enthroned in heaven." There, the direction of hope is unambiguous—*toward* God. Here, by contrast, the lifting is *toward* mountains whose identity and significance remain, for one verse, tantalizingly unclear. This dramatic tension sets up the psalm's theological resolution in verse 2.

---

## Verse 2

**עֶזְרִי מֵעִם יְהוָה, עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ.**

The answer arrives with decisive clarity: "My help comes from YHWH, maker of heaven and earth." The preposition shifts from אֶל (toward) to מֵעִם (from with/beside), emphasizing that help's *source* is not the mountains themselves but the God who transcends all geography. The creedal formula עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ (oseh shamayim va-aretz, "maker of heaven and earth") is a fixed liturgical phrase appearing in Psalms 115:15, 124:8, 134:3, and 146:6, always in contexts emphasizing God's absolute sovereignty. Genesis 14:19 uses the variant קֹנֵה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ ("creator/possessor of heaven and earth") in Melchizedek's blessing of Abram, and Exodus 20:11 grounds the Sabbath commandment in this same cosmogonic authority: "כִּי שֵׁשֶׁת־יָמִים עָשָׂה יְהוָה אֶת־הַשָּׁמַיִם וְאֶת־הָאָרֶץ—For in six days YHWH made the heavens and the earth."

This is not merely a pious cliché. The phrase functions polemically, asserting that the pilgrim's help comes not from any localized mountain deity (like Baal of Ṣapānu or the gods of Bashan in Psalm 68:16) but from the universal Creator. As Radak notes: "וְהַכֹּל בְּיָדוֹ לַעֲשׂוֹת—Everything is in His power to do." The merism "heaven and earth" (a polar pair expressing totality, like "day and night" or "east and west") encompasses *all* that exists—there is no realm, no force, no threat outside the Creator's dominion.

The phrase appears with particular frequency at structural boundaries. Psalm 124:8 uses it as a *conclusio*: "עֶזְרֵנוּ בְּשֵׁם יְהוָה עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ—Our help is in the name of YHWH, maker of heaven and earth," framing a psalm of national deliverance. Psalm 134:3 employs it as a priestly benediction: "יְבָרֶכְךָ יְהוָה מִצִּיּוֹן עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ—May YHWH bless you from Zion, maker of heaven and earth," closing the entire Songs of Ascents collection. Here, in Psalm 121:2, it resolves the opening question with a cosmological declaration: whatever threats the mountains may pose, they are subsumed under the sovereignty of their Maker.

The liturgical afterlife of this verse is revealing. In Ashkenazic tradition, it appears as a congregational response in the *Kaddish Shalem* (Full Kaddish), recited immediately after the Aramaic petition: "יְהֵא שְׁלָמָא רַבָּא מִן שְׁמַיָא וְחַיִּים עָלֵינוּ... וְאִמְרוּ אָמֵן: עֶזְרִי מֵעִם יְהוָה עֹשֵׂה שָׁמַיִם וָאָרֶץ—May there be abundant peace from heaven and life upon us... and say Amen: My help is from YHWH, Maker of heaven and earth." This liturgical placement transforms a personal confession into a communal affirmation, grounding the prayer for peace in the unshakeable foundation of cosmic creation.

---

## Verse 3

**אַל־יִתֵּן לַמּוֹט רַגְלֶךָ; אַל־יָנוּם, שֹׁמְרֶךָ.**

At this pivotal juncture, the psalm executes its most dramatic literary move: a shift from first-person confession (vv. 1–2: "I lift my eyes... my help") to second-person priestly assurance (vv. 3–8: "your foot... your guardian... you"). This is not a stylistic accident but a dialogical structure reflecting actual liturgical performance. As Metzudat David explains: "כְּאִלּוּ אֶחָד יֹאמַר לַחֲבֵרוֹ דֶּרֶךְ נֶחָמָה הִנֵּה עֶזְרָה טוֹבָה הִיא—It is as if one says to another, by way of comfort, 'Behold, help is good.'" The pilgrim has voiced existential anxiety; now a priest (or temple attendant, or the congregation itself) responds with authoritative blessing.

The verse opens with two emphatic negations employing the particle אַל (al) with the imperfect, creating strong jussive force: "He will not let... he will not slumber." The first prohibition addresses the specific, physical danger of pilgrimage: "אַל־יִתֵּן לַמּוֹט רַגְלֶךָ—He will not let your foot give way." The verb מוֹט (mot) appears 39 times in the Hebrew Bible, predominantly in the Psalms, with the basic meaning of "totter, slip, shake" (BDB, 556). It is both literal (dangerous mountain paths where a misstep could be fatal) and metaphorical (spiritual or moral instability). Psalm 15:5 promises that the righteous person "לֹא יִמּוֹט לְעוֹלָם—shall never be moved," using the same root to describe covenantal security. Here, the foot (רֶגֶל, regel) stands metonymically for the whole person and the whole pilgrimage—physical, spiritual, and communal.

The second prohibition introduces the dominant motif of the entire psalm: שֹׁמְרֶךָ (shomrekha, "your guardian"). This is the first of *six* appearances of the root שׁ-מ-ר (sh-m-r, "guard, keep, watch") in eight verses (vv. 3, 3, 4, 5, 7, 7, 8). The verb שָׁמַר (shamar) carries rich covenantal resonance throughout Scripture. In Genesis 2:15, the human is placed in Eden "לְעָבְדָהּ וּלְשָׁמְרָהּ—to work it and to guard it"; in the Shema (Deuteronomy 6:17), Israel is commanded "שָׁמוֹר תִּשְׁמְרוּן—you shall diligently keep" God's commandments. The term denotes not passive watching but active, vigilant, covenant-faithful protection.

The assertion "אַל־יָנוּם שֹׁמְרֶךָ—He will not slumber, your guardian" employs the verb נוּם (num), a relatively rare term appearing only six times in the Hebrew Bible (BDB, 630). It denotes a light sleep or dozing, as distinct from full sleep (יָשֵׁן, yashen). The pairing of the two verbs in verse 4 (לֹא־יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן, "neither slumbers nor sleeps") creates an emphatic hendiadys—two terms expressing a single, intensified idea. This is likely polemical. In 1 Kings 18:27, the prophet Elijah mocks the priests of Baal: "קִרְאוּ בְקוֹל־גָּדוֹל כִּי־אֱלֹהִים הוּא... אוּלַי יָשֵׁן הוּא וְיִקָץ—Cry aloud, for he is a god... perhaps he is asleep and must be awakened." The Ugaritic Baal cycle depicts the storm god's seasonal death and slumber. Against this backdrop, the assertion that YHWH "does not slumber" is not poetic flourish but theological polemic: Israel's God, unlike the dying-and-rising vegetation deities of Canaan, maintains perpetual vigilance.

---

## Verse 4

**הִנֵּה לֹא־יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן, שֹׁמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל.**

The particle הִנֵּה (hinneh, "behold, look!") demands attention, marking what follows as particularly significant. It appears over 1,000 times in the Hebrew Bible, frequently to introduce divine speech or to underscore a dramatic revelation. Here it amplifies the doubled negation already introduced in verse 3, now with both verbs explicitly stated: "לֹא־יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן—neither slumbers nor sleeps." As the Meiri observes: "הַתְּנוּמָה הִיא שֵׁינָה קַלָּה וְהַשֵּׁינָה שֵׁינָה גְּמוּרָה—Slumber is light sleep and sleep is deep sleep." The progression from lighter to heavier sleep creates a rhetorical climax, asserting that God's watchfulness is absolutely uninterrupted.

This verse makes a crucial theological move by shifting from the individual to the collective: "שֹׁמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל—the guardian of Israel." The personal "your guardian" (שֹׁמְרֶךָ) of verse 3 is now revealed to be a function of God's covenantal relationship with the entire people. The individual pilgrim's protection is not an isolated divine act but participates in God's larger shomrim (guardianship) of Israel as a covenantal community. This pattern—moving from personal address to collective identity and back to personal—creates a dynamic interplay. As Rashi notes, the sleeplessness of Israel's guardian is demonstrated in the exile: "כִּי בִהְיוֹת הַגָּלוּת דּוֹמֶה לְיָשֵׁן—For while the exile endures, it seems as if [God] sleeps," yet the promise holds that God's vigilance never ceases.

The phrase "guardian of Israel" (שֹׁמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל) became so central to Jewish consciousness that it entered the daily liturgy in multiple forms. It appears in the bedtime Shema (K'riat Shema Al HaMitah) after the Priestly Blessing: "יִשָּׂא יְהוָה פָּנָיו אֵלֶיךָ וְיָשֵׂם לְךָ שָׁלוֹם: הִנֵּה לֹא יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן שׁוֹמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל—May YHWH lift His face toward you and grant you peace: Behold, the Guardian of Israel neither slumbers nor sleeps." The pairing with the Priestly Blessing reframes divine watchfulness as continuous benediction. Intriguingly, the verse also appears embedded in the Ashkenazic Yom Kippur Vidui (confessional), where—amid calls for national repentance—it reassures that God's watchful care persists even in judgment: "אַל תִּירָא יַעֲקֹב... שׁוּבָה יִשְׂרָאֵל: הִנֵּה לֹא יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן שׁוֹמֵר יִשְׂרָאֵל: כַּכָּתוּב... שׁוּבָה יִשְׂרָאֵל עַד יְהוָה אֱלֹהֶיךָ—Fear not, Jacob... Return, Israel: Behold, the Guardian of Israel neither slumbers nor sleeps: As it is written... Return, Israel, to YHWH your God" (Hosea 14:2).

The polemic against sleeping gods finds its clearest biblical expression in Isaiah 5:27, which describes the coming army that God will summon: "אֵין־עָיֵף וְאֵין־כּוֹשֵׁל בּוֹ, לֹא יָנוּם וְלֹא יִישָׁן—None is weary, none stumbles among them; none slumbers or sleeps." Here, the denial of sleep applies to God's *agents*, but the theological implication is clear: a God who neither slumbers nor sleeps deploys forces that inherit divine vigilance. The contrast with ancient Near Eastern mythology is stark. In the Akkadian *Atrahasis Epic*, the gods are disturbed by human noise and send a flood because they cannot sleep; in the Babylonian *Enuma Elish*, Apsu's sleep is a vulnerability exploited by Ea. The God of Israel requires no sleep, admits no vulnerability, and maintains perpetual, attentive care over the covenant people.

---

## Verse 5

**יְהוָה שֹׁמְרֶךָ; יְהוָה צִלְּךָ, עַל־יַד יְמִינֶךָ.**

The divine name יְהוָה (YHWH) appears *twice* in this verse, a striking anaphora (repetition at the beginning of successive clauses) that emphasizes the personal, covenantal relationship between God and the worshiper. This is "second-person theology"—not abstract discourse about God but direct address *to* the individual: "YHWH is *your* guardian; YHWH is *your* shade." The repetition of the Tetragrammaton five times in verses 5, 7, and 8 creates a sonic and theological drumbeat, asserting divine presence with increasing intensity.

The first colon reprises the שׁ-מ-ר motif: "יְהוָה שֹׁמְרֶךָ—YHWH is your guardian." The second colon introduces a new metaphor: "יְהוָה צִלְּךָ—YHWH is your shade." The noun צֵל (tsel, "shadow, shade") appears 53 times in the Hebrew Bible with multiple valences. Literally, shade is essential protection from the sun's lethal heat in the ancient Near Eastern climate (compare Jonah 4:5–6, where Jonah sits "בַּצֵּל עַד אֲשֶׁר יִרְאֶה מַה־יִּהְיֶה בָּעִיר—in the shade until he should see what would happen to the city"). Metaphorically, shade represents any protective covering. In the Song of Songs 2:3, the beloved declares: "בְּצִלּוֹ חִמַּדְתִּי וְיָשַׁבְתִּי—In his shade I delighted to sit," evoking romantic shelter. Isaiah 25:4 praises God: "כִּי־הָיִיתָ מָעוֹז... צֵל מֵחֹרֶב—For You have been a stronghold... shade from heat."

The most potent biblical shade imagery is that of *God's shadow*. Psalm 91:1 opens with this declaration: "יֹשֵׁב בְּסֵתֶר עֶלְיוֹן בְּצֵל שַׁדַּי יִתְלוֹנָן—Whoever dwells in the shelter of the Most High will lodge in the shadow of the Almighty," where "shadow" (צֵל) is paralleled with "shelter" (סֵתֶר), emphasizing comprehensive divine protection. Psalm 17:8 intensifies this: "הַסְתִּירֵנִי בְּצֵל כְּנָפֶיךָ—Hide me in the shadow of Your wings," where the shade becomes avian, suggesting the hovering, protective presence of a mother bird (cf. Deuteronomy 32:11's eagle imagery).

The phrase "עַל־יַד יְמִינֶךָ—at your right hand" is richly multivalent. Ibn Ezra understands it militarily: the right hand is the weapon side, and the phrase "suggests military protection—the right hand being the weapon side, vulnerable in combat. The divine warrior stations himself as shield at the fighter's exposed flank." This reading finds support in Psalm 110:5: "אֲדֹנָי עַל־יְמִינְךָ מָחַץ בְּיוֹם־אַפּוֹ מְלָכִים—The Lord is at your right hand; He crushes kings in the day of His wrath," where God's position at the right hand is explicitly martial. Alternatively, the right hand may evoke legal or social contexts—the place of honor and advocacy. Psalm 16:8 declares: "שִׁוִּיתִי יְהוָה לְנֶגְדִּי תָמִיד כִּי מִימִינִי בַּל־אֶמּוֹט—I have set YHWH before me always; because He is at my right hand, I shall not be moved." Here, the right-hand position ensures stability and prevents "moving" (מוֹט)—recalling the "not letting the foot give way" of verse 3.

In modern liturgy, this verse finds a particularly poignant application. In the Sefardic rite for *Pidyon HaBen* (Redemption of the Firstborn Son), these protective verses are recited by the Kohen as blessings for the newly redeemed infant: "יְהוָה שֹׁמְרֶךָ יְהוָה צִלְּךָ עַל יַד יְמִינֶךָ... יְהוָה יִשְׁמָרְךָ מִכָּל־רָע יִשְׁמֹר אֶת־נַפְשֶׁךָ—The LORD is your guardian, the LORD is your shade at your right hand... The LORD will guard you from all evil; He will guard your soul." The selection of this text for a life-cycle ritual underscores its function as a comprehensive prayer for protection from infancy onward.

---

## Verse 6

**יוֹמָם הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ לֹא־יַכֶּכָּה, וְיָרֵחַ בַּלָּיְלָה.**

This verse operationalizes the "shade" metaphor of verse 5, specifying the celestial threats from which God protects: "By day the sun will not strike you, nor the moon by night." The structure is a merism—a figure of speech where two opposite poles represent the entirety of a spectrum. "Day and night" (יוֹמָם... בַּלָּיְלָה, yomam... ba-laylah) encompasses the full 24-hour cycle, asserting that divine protection is temporally comprehensive and uninterrupted. Genesis 8:22 uses the same device: "יוֹם וָלַיְלָה לֹא יִשְׁבֹּתוּ—Day and night shall not cease," describing the restored cosmic order after the flood. Similarly, Psalm 55:11 laments: "יוֹמָם וָלַיְלָה יְסוֹבְבֻהָ עַל־חוֹמֹתֶיהָ—Day and night they make their rounds on its walls," depicting continuous enemy activity.

The sun's danger is obvious: in the ancient Near East, sunstroke (יַכֶּכָּה, yakkekah, from נָכָה, nakah, "to strike, smite") was a real and often fatal threat to travelers in the open. Job 30:28 describes the sufferer's condition: "קֹדֵר הִלַּכְתִּי בְּלֹא חַמָּה—I go about blackened, but not by the sun," implying that sun-blackening was a common affliction. Jonah 4:8 recounts God appointing "רוּחַ קָדִים חֲרִישִׁית וַתַּךְ הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ עַל־רֹאשׁ יוֹנָה—a sultry east wind, and the sun beat down on Jonah's head," driving him to despair. The verb נָכָה (to strike, smite) appears 501 times in the Hebrew Bible, predominantly in military contexts (Deuteronomy 7:2: "וְהִכִּיתָם הַחֲרֵם תַּחֲרִים אֹתָם—you shall strike them down, you shall utterly destroy them"), but also in natural contexts like plague (Exodus 9:15: "וָאַךְ אֹתְךָ בַּדֶּבֶר—I struck you with pestilence") and divine judgment (Psalm 102:5: "הֻכָּה כָעֵשֶׂב וַיִּבַשׁ לִבִּי—My heart is stricken and withered like grass").

The moon's threat is more enigmatic. The phrase "וְיָרֵחַ בַּלָּיְלָה—nor the moon by night" lacks an explicit verb, which must be supplied from the first colon: "לֹא־יַכֶּכָּה... [וְיָרֵחַ בַּלָּיְלָה לֹא־יַכֶּכָּה]—will not strike you... [nor will the moon by night strike you]." But how does the moon "strike"? Ancient beliefs about lunar influence ranged from medical (the moon was thought to cause or exacerbate mental illness—hence "lunacy," from Latin *luna*, "moon") to demonic (the moon as a time when malevolent spiritual forces were active). Isaiah 13:10, in an oracle of cosmic judgment, declares: "לֹא־יָהֵל כּוֹכָב בִּנְשָׁפוֹ וְחָשַׁךְ הַיָּרֵחַ—The stars will not shine their light, and the moon will be dark," where the moon is one of the celestial powers subject to divine control. More directly, Isaiah 30:26 describes eschatological healing: "וְהָיָה אוֹר־הַלְּבָנָה כְּאוֹר הַחַמָּה וְאוֹר הַחַמָּה יִהְיֶה שִׁבְעָתַיִם—The light of the moon shall be as the light of the sun, and the light of the sun shall be sevenfold," suggesting that *both* sun and moon were understood as powerful forces requiring divine moderation.

Radak offers a medical interpretation rooted in humoral theory: "כִּי חֹם הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ יְיַבֵּשׁ הַלֵּחָה וְהַיָּרֵחַ יוֹסִיף הַלַּחְלָחָה יִהְיֶה מִמֶּנָּה הַקְּרִירוּת, וְהַחֹם וְהַקֹּר הֵם סִבַּת הַתַּחֲלוּאִים וְהָאֵל יִתְבָּרַךְ יִשְׁמֹר מֵהֶם—For the sun's heat dries the moisture [of the body], and the moon increases moisture, from which comes coldness, and heat and cold are the cause of diseases, and God, blessed be He, will guard from them." This reflects the ancient understanding that disease resulted from imbalances in the body's hot/cold, wet/dry humors, which celestial bodies could influence.

More theologically, the sun and moon were primary objects of pagan worship in the ancient Near East. The Mesopotamian pantheon included *Shamash* (sun god, cognate with Hebrew שֶׁמֶשׁ) and *Sin* (moon god, perhaps related to the name Sinai). Deuteronomy 4:19 warns Israel: "וּפֶן־תִּשָּׂא עֵינֶיךָ הַשָּׁמַיְמָה... וְהִשְׁתַּחֲוִיתָ לָהֶם וַעֲבַדְתָּם—And lest you lift your eyes to heaven... and worship them and serve them," referring to sun, moon, and stars. By asserting that *YHWH* protects from the sun and moon, Psalm 121 subtly demythologizes these celestial bodies: they are not gods to be feared or worshiped but created elements under God's sovereign control. As Ibn Ezra tersely notes: "הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ תַּזִּיק בְּרֹב הַחֹם שֶׁלָּהּ וְהַלְּבָנָה בַּלַּיְלָה תּוֹסִיף לֵחָה מוֹלֶדֶת תַּחֲלוּאִים, וְזֶה הַדָּבָר בָּרוּר וּמְנֻסֶּה—The sun harms through its excessive heat, and the moon by night increases moisture that generates diseases, and this matter is clear and tested," treating them as natural phenomena, not divine powers.

---

## Verse 7

**יְהוָה יִשְׁמָרְךָ מִכָּל־רָע; יִשְׁמֹר אֶת־נַפְשֶׁךָ.**

The sixth and seventh occurrences of the root שׁ-מ-ר appear in quick succession: "יִשְׁמָרְךָ... יִשְׁמֹר—will guard you... will guard." This intensifying repetition (anadiplosis, or "staircase parallelism," where a word from the first colon is picked up and developed in the second) propels the psalm toward its climax. The double use of שָׁמַר in a single verse underscores the emphatic, comprehensive nature of divine protection being proclaimed.

The first colon asserts: "יְהוָה יִשְׁמָרְךָ מִכָּל־רָע—YHWH will guard you from all evil." The phrase מִכָּל־רָע (mikol-ra, "from all evil") is maximally inclusive, leaving no category of harm unaddressed. The term רָע (ra) has an extraordinary semantic range in biblical Hebrew, encompassing moral evil (Genesis 6:5: "רַעַת הָאָדָם—the wickedness of humanity"), physical disaster (Amos 3:6: "הֲיִהְיֶה רָעָה בְּעִיר וַיהוָה לֹא עָשָׂה—Does disaster befall a city unless YHWH has done it?"), emotional distress (Genesis 44:34: "פֶּן־אֶרְאֶה בָרָע אֲשֶׁר יִמְצָא אֶת־אָבִי—lest I see the evil that would come upon my father"), and enemy action (Psalm 23:4: "לֹא־אִירָא רָע—I will fear no evil").

The comprehensiveness of מִכָּל־רָע finds a close parallel in Psalm 91, another psalm of protection. There, verse 10 promises: "לֹא־תְאֻנֶּה אֵלֶיךָ רָעָה—No evil shall befall you," and verse 3 specifies protection "מִדֶּבֶר הַוּוֹת מִדֶּבֶר הַוּוֹת—from the deadly pestilence." The absence of qualifiers or exceptions in Psalm 121:7's "all evil" creates a rhetorical absolute. As Radak comments: "שֶׁלֹּא יַזִּיקוּךָ בְּנֵי אָדָם וְחַיּוֹת הַשָּׂדֶה... שֶׁלֹּא תָמוּת מִתּוֹךְ הַחֳלָאִים—So that people will not harm you, nor wild animals... so that you will not die from diseases," reading רָע as encompassing every conceivable threat—human, animal, and natural.

The second colon narrows from the comprehensive to the essential: "יִשְׁמֹר אֶת־נַפְשֶׁךָ—He will guard your *nefesh*." The noun נֶפֶשׁ (nefesh) is notoriously difficult to translate precisely. BDB (659-661) lists its meanings as "soul, living being, life, self, person, desire, passion, appetite, emotion." It can refer to physical life (Genesis 9:5: "אַךְ־אֶת־דִּמְכֶם לְנַפְשֹׁתֵיכֶם—your lifeblood I will require"), the essential self (Deuteronomy 6:5: "בְּכָל־נַפְשְׁךָ—with all your soul"), or even serve as a reflexive pronoun (Genesis 27:25: "הַגִּשָׁה לִּי וְאֹכְלָה מִצֵּיד בְּנִי לְמַעַן תְּבָרֶכְךָ נַפְשִׁי—Bring it to me and I will eat of my son's game, that *I myself* may bless you").

Here, the shift from guarding "you" (יִשְׁמָרְךָ) to guarding "your נֶפֶשׁ" (יִשְׁמֹר אֶת־נַפְשֶׁךָ) suggests a movement from external to internal, from circumstantial protection to preservation of the essential life force. The Meiri understands this as dual protection: "וְהִבְטִיחוֹ בִּשְׁמִירַת הַגּוּף וּשְׁמִירַת הַנֶּפֶשׁ—And He assured him with protection of the body and protection of the soul," interpreting רָע as physical harm and נֶפֶשׁ as the spiritual essence that must also be guarded.

Liturgically, this verse has become a staple of protective prayer in Jewish tradition. In the Sefardic home ritual for Shabbat, following *Shalom Aleichem* (the song welcoming Sabbath angels), verses from Psalm 121 are recited, including: "יוֹמָם הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ לֹא יַכֶּכָּה וְיָרֵחַ בַּלָּיְלָה: יְהֹוָה יִשְׁמְרְךָ מִכָּל רָע יִשְׁמֹר אֶת־נַפְשֶׁךָ: יְהֹוָה יִשְׁמָר צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ מֵעַתָּה וְעַד עוֹלָם—By day the sun shall not strike you, nor the moon by night: The LORD will guard you from all evil; He will guard your soul: The LORD will guard your going out and your coming in, from now and forever." This custom, recited at the threshold between sacred Sabbath time and the coming week's activities, frames the home as a space of perpetual divine guardianship.

---

## Verse 8

**יְהוָה יִשְׁמָר־צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ, מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם.**

The psalm concludes with a sixth and final use of שָׁמַר, now in a comprehensive blessing formula that achieves total scope through two merismus pairs: "יְהוָה יִשְׁמָר־צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ—YHWH will guard your going out and your coming in" (spatial/activity totality), "מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם—from now and forever" (temporal totality). The verse thus constructs a rhetorical fortress: no space, no time, no activity falls outside the scope of divine protection.

The phrase "צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ" (tset'kha u-vo'ekha, "your going out and your coming in") is a fixed merism appearing throughout the Hebrew Bible. Its most famous occurrence is in the Deuteronomic blessing-curse formula. Deuteronomy 28:6 promises the obedient: "בָּרוּךְ אַתָּה בְּבֹאֶךָ וּבָרוּךְ אַתָּה בְּצֵאתֶךָ—Blessed shall you be in your coming in, and blessed shall you be in your going out," while verse 19 warns the disobedient: "אָרוּר אַתָּה בְּבֹאֶךָ וְאָרוּר אַתָּה בְּצֵאתֶךָ—Cursed shall you be in your coming in, and cursed shall you be in your going out." The merism can denote military activity: Numbers 27:17 describes the need for a leader "אֲשֶׁר־יֵצֵא לִפְנֵיהֶם וַאֲשֶׁר יָבֹא לִפְנֵיהֶם—who shall go out before them and who shall come in before them," referring to military campaigns. More generally, it signifies the totality of life's activities and movements. Joshua 14:11 uses it comprehensively: "כְּכֹחִי אָז וּכְכֹחִי עַתָּה לַמִּלְחָמָה וְלָצֵאת וְלָבוֹא—My strength then was as my strength is now, for war and for going out and coming in."

In Psalm 121:8, the phrase functions at multiple levels. For the original pilgrimage context, it most obviously refers to the journey itself: going out from one's hometown and coming in to Jerusalem, then the return journey (going out from Jerusalem and coming in to home). Radak reads it this way: "בְּצֵאתְךָ מֵהַגָּלוּת וּבְבוֹאֲךָ לְאֶרֶץ יִשְׂרָאֵל תָּבוֹא בְשָׁלוֹם—In your going out from the exile and your coming in to the land of Israel, you will come in peace." But the phrase's conventional use as a merism for all activity means it extends far beyond literal travel: every departure and arrival, every beginning and ending, every enterprise undertaken and completed.

The temporal merism "מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם" (me-atah ve-ad-olam, "from now and forever") appears multiple times in the Psalter, often at psalm conclusions. Psalm 113:2, in a doxology, declares: "יְהִי שֵׁם יְהוָה מְבֹרָךְ מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם—Let the name of YHWH be blessed from now and forever." Psalm 115:18, at the climax of a psalm praising God's sovereignty, proclaims: "וַאֲנַחְנוּ נְבָרֵךְ יָהּ מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם הַלְלוּ־יָהּ—But we will bless YAH from now and forever. Hallelujah!" Psalm 125:2, another Song of Ascents, uses it to describe God's protection: "יְרוּשָׁלַם הָרִים סָבִיב לָהּ וַיהוָה סָבִיב לְעַמּוֹ מֵעַתָּה וְעַד־עוֹלָם—Jerusalem, hills enfold it, and YHWH enfolds His people from now and forever."

The term עוֹלָם (olam) presents a fascinating semantic complexity. BDB (761-763) defines it as "long duration, antiquity, futurity," with meanings spanning from remote past to indefinite future to eternity. In pre-exilic texts, it often denotes "as long as one lives" or "a very long time" (1 Samuel 1:22: "וְיָשַׁב שָׁם עַד־עוֹלָם—and remain there forever," meaning during Samuel's lifetime). In later texts, particularly those influenced by apocalyptic thought, it takes on the meaning of "eternity" in a metaphysical sense. Psalm 121:8's "וְעַד־עוֹלָם" functions as a comprehensive temporal boundary—whether understood as "as long as life endures" or "into eternity," it asserts that divine protection has no expiration date.

Liturgically, this verse has become ubiquitous as a blessing formula. In *Tefillat HaDerech*, the Traveler's Prayer, supplementary verses invoke protection: "כִּי מַלְאָכָיו יְצַוֶּה לָךְ לִשְׁמָרְךָ בְּכָל דְרָכֶיךָ: יי יִשְׁמָר צֵאתְךָ וּבוֹאֶךָ מֵעַתָּה וְעַד עוֹלָם—For He will command His angels concerning you, to guard you in all your ways: YHWH will guard your going out and your coming in from now and forever" (Psalm 91:11 paired with Psalm 121:8). The pairing links angelic and divine guardianship, human vulnerability and cosmic protection, specific journey and eternal care.

The psalm ends, as it began, with a question—but the question has been transformed. Verse 1 asked in uncertainty: "מֵאַיִן יָבֹא עֶזְרִי—From where will my help come?" Verse 8 provides a multidimensional answer: help comes from YHWH (v. 2), who is maker of heaven and earth (v. 2), who neither slumbers nor sleeps (v. 4), who is guardian of Israel (v. 4) and your personal guardian (v. 5), your shade at your right hand (v. 5), who protects from sun and moon (v. 6), from all evil (v. 7), who guards your *nefesh* (v. 7), your going out and coming in (v. 8), from this moment until the end of time (v. 8). The cumulative effect is overwhelming—a piling up of protective assertions, a systematic dismantling of every possible source of anxiety. The pilgrim who began by lifting eyes to ambiguous mountains ends enfolded in a comprehensive shield of divine care, temporally infinite and spatially all-encompassing. The journey to Jerusalem becomes a metaphor for the journey of faith itself: from uncertainty to trust, from looking to mountains to looking to their Maker, from "where?" to "from YHWH."

### מֵאַיִן
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אַ֫יִן⁴  

cstr. אֵין subst. proposes nothing, nought (Moab. אן, As. iûnu). †Is 40:23 הַנּוֹתֵן רוֹזְנִים לְאָ֑יִן who bringeth princes to nothing; †כְּאַיִן as nothing, ib. 40:17; 41:11, 12 Hg 2:3 ψ 39:6; almost (‖ כִּמְעַט) ψ 73:2; †מֵאַיִן of nothing Is 41:24. cstr. אֵין, very freq. as particle of negation, is not, are not, was not, were not, etc. (corresp. to the affirm. יֵשׁ q.v. Similar in usage, though not etym. akin, are لَيْسَ, לַיִת, ܠܰܝܬ, አልቦ), proposes ‘there is nought of …’ sq. a subst. or a pron. suffix (אֵינֶנִּי [verbal form, Ges§ 100, 5; 152, 1 c], אֵינְךָ, אֵינֵךְ, אֵינֶנּוּ, אֵינֶנָּה, אֵינְכֶם, אֵינָם, also ψ 59:14 אֵינֵ֫מוֹ, 73:5 אֵינֵ֫ימוֹ): twice abnormally, in late Heb., a nom. אֵין אֲנִי, אֵין אֲנַחְנוּ Ne 4:17 (so sts. לַיִת, ܠܰܝܬ, NöM. p. 295); once, incorrectly, את Hg 2:17 (?). denying existence absolutely Is 44:6; 47:10 אֵין רֹאָנִי there is none that seeth me, lit. nought of one seeing me! אֵין עוֹד there is none else Dt 4:39 1 K 8:60 Is 45:5, 6, 18, 22. more commonly, in a limited sense, there is none here or at hand Ex 2:12 and he saw כִּי־אֵין אִישׁ that there was no man (sc. there), Nu 21:5; Gn 5:24 וְאֵינֶנּוּ and he was not (of Enoch’s disappearance from earth) 42:13 one (cas.pend. as oft.), he is not, v 36; oft. = is (or has) vanished Gn 37:30 1 K 20:40 Is 17:14 ψ 37:10; 39:14; 103:16 Jb 8:22; 24:24; 27:19. with the sense determined by a predic. following: Gn 37:29 Joseph was not in the pit, 41:39 + often; Ex 5:10 אֵינֶנִּי נֹתֵן לָכֶם תֶּבֶן I am not giving you straw; and so often with particip. where duration has to be expressed Gn 39:23 Dt 21:18 Is 1:15 Je 7:16, or intention Gn 20:7 אִם אֵינְךָ מֵשִׁיב דַּע … if thou art not restoring her, know, 43:5 Ex 8:17; 33:15 (idiomatically, after אם; v. Dr§ 137) Ju 12:3. Foll. once pleon. by יֵשׁ ψ 135:17. Treated as a mere part. of negation, אֵין may vary its position in the sentence, the subst. which should strictly stand in the genitive being not only separated from it by a little word, as בּוֹ Gn 37:24, לוֹ Ex 22:1, שָׁם Ex 12:30, גַּם ψ 14:3, etc., but even for emphasis prefixed to it, as Gn 19:31; 40:8 וּפֹתֵר אֵין אֹתוֹ 41:15 Ex 5:16 Ju 13:9; 14:6; 16:15; 19:1 (so MI 24): if however it be thus brought to the end of a sentence, or be disconnected with what follows, it stands in the absol. form, as Gn 2:5 ואדם אַיִן לעבד האדמה and man there was none to till the ground, Lv 26:37 וְרֹדֵף אָ֑יִן, Nu 20:5 2 K 19:3 Ho 13:4 Mi 7:2. sometimes the subj. has to be supplied from the context: thus (α) †1 S 9:4 and they passed through the land of Shaalim וָאַיִן and they (the asses) were not (lit. and nought!), especially after vbs. of waiting or seeking Is 59:11 ψ 69:21 Jb 3:9; Is 41:17 Ez 7:25 Pr 14:6; 13:4; 20:4. (β) †Ex 17:7 is י׳ in the midst of us אִם־אָֽיִן׃ or not? Nu 13:20. (γ) †Ju 4:20 וְאָמַרְתְּ אָֽיִן׃ then thou shalt say, There is not, 1 K 18:10 1 S 10:14. (δ) Gn 30:1 give me children, וְאִם־אַיִן and if not, I die, Ex 32:32 Ju 9:15, 20 2 S 17:6 (v. Dr) 2 K 2:10 Jb 33:33. with subj. not expressed, once (late), Dn 8:5 וְאֵין נוֹגֵעַ בָּאָרֶץ and (it) was not touching the earth. Ec 8:11, Jb 35:15 with the finite vb.; but rd. here כִּי אֵין פֹּקֵד (the usage of لَيْسَ, cited by De, does not justify the anomaly in Heb.) Je 38:5 the impf. may be due to the fact that no ptcp. of יָכֹל was in use, and a relat. must be tacitly supplied: ‘The king is not (one that) can do aught against you.’ On Ex 3:2 see Ges§ 52, 2 R. 6; Ew§ 169 d. אֵין לְ׳, with subst., or pron., there is (was) not to … = … have, has, had, etc. not: Gn 11:30 אֵין לָהּ וָלָד she had no child, Nu 27:9 וְאִם־אֵין לוֹ בַּת and if he have no daughter + oft.; with a ptcp. Dt 22:27 Je 14:16; 30:17; 49:5; 50:32 ψ 142:5 La 1:2, 9, 17; Ex 22:2 אִם־אֵין לוֹ if he have nought, Dn 9:26 ואין לו and have nought (or none). in circumst. clauses (Dr§ 164):—(a) Ex 21:11 she shall go out free אֵין כָּ֑סֶף without money, 22:9 אֵין רֹאֶה none seeing it, Nu 11:6 Is 47:1 Je 2:32 Ho 3:4; 7:11 ψ 32:9; 88:5 +. (b) Dt 32:4 a God of faithfulness וְאֵין עָוֶל and no iniquity, i.e. without iniquity, Je 5:21 Jo 1:6 ψ 104:25. (c) very oft., in such phrases as וְאֵין מַחֲרִיד with none to affright Lv 26:6 (12 textual); וְאֵין מְכַבֶּה Is 1:31 al.; וְאֵין מַצִּיל 5:29 ψ 7:3, etc. (Dr§ 159). with inf. and ל׳, it is not to …: i.e. (α) like οὐκ ἔστιν, it is not possible to … (cf. sub יֵשׁ and לֹא), but hardly exc. in late Heb.; 2 Ch 20:6 וְאֵין עִמְּךָ לְהִתְיַצֵּב it is not possible to stand (in conflict) with thee, 22:9 Ezr 9:15 Ec 3:14 Est 4:2. Once without ל, ψ 40:6 אֵין עֲרֹךְ אֵלֶיךָ οὐκ ἔστι παραβάλλειν σοι. (β) there is no need to … 1 Ch 23:26 וְגַם לַלְוִיִּם אֵין־לָשֵׂאת for the Levites also there was no need to bear 2 Ch 5:11; 35:15 (v. Dr§ 202. 1). with prefixes:— †בְּאֵין prop. in defect of:—(α) for want of, without—Pr 5:23 he will die בְּאֵין מוּסָר for lack of instruction, 11:14 בְּאֵין תַּחְבֻּלוֹת without guidance, 14:4; 15:22; 26:20; 29:18 Is 57:1 Ez 38:11; cf. בְּלֹא. (β) of time = when there was (were) not Pr 8:24(×2). †כְּאֵין Is 59:10 כְּאֵין עֵינַיִם poet. for כַּאֲשֶׁר אֵין לוֹ עֵינַיִם (cf. Ew§ 286 g Ges§ 152. 1 h). †לְאֵין (α) for לַאֲשֶׁר אֵין לוֹ Is 40:29; in late prose 2 Ch 14:10 Ne 8:10. (β) in the condition of not … (ל of state, v. sub ל) = without or so that not … (peculiar to Ch), 1 Ch 22:4 cedar trees לְאֵין מִסְפָּר without number, 2 Ch 14:12 and there fell of the Cushites לְאֵין־לָהֶם מִחְיָה so that they had none remaining alive, 20:25 לְאֵין מַשָּׂא so that there was no carrying away, 21:18 Ezr 9:14. (γ) עַד־לְאֵין (see עַד ל׳), 2 Ch 36:16 until there was no remedy (cf. עַד אֵין … ψ 40:13 Jb 5:9). מֵאֵין (α) (מִן causal) from lack of … Is 50:2 Je 7:32; 19:11 Ez 34:8. (β) (v. מִן 7 b) proposes away from there being no … (with אֵין pleon., cf. מִבְּלִי, and מִבְּלִי אֵין), i.e. so that not …, without, mostly epexegetical of some term expressing desolation: Is 5:9 Surely many houses shall be desolate מֵאֵין יוֹשֵׁב without inhabitant, 6:11 + oft. Je & Zp; Is 6:11 מֵאֵין אָדָם, Je 32:43 מֵאֵין אָדָם וּבְהֵמָה 33:10, 12 Ez 33:28; La 3:49. Once sq. inf. Mal 2:13 so that there is no regarding more. (γ) in Je 10:6, 7 מֵאֵין כָּמוֹךָ, מֵאֵין is supposed by some to = a strengthened אֵין, even none, none at all; but it is difficult to justify this expl. logically; and it is preferable to point מֵאַיִן כָּמוֹךָ whence is any like thee? cf. 30:7. (So Mich v. DrHbr ii.34–7.)

### מֵאַיִן

**Vocalized**: אָן²  

adv. v. p. 33 sub אַי.

### עֶזְרִי
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עֵ֫זֶר  

n.m. ψ 121:1 help, succour;—ע׳ abs. Gn 2:18 +, sf. עֶזְרִי Ex 18:4, עֶזְרֹה Ez 12:14, etc.;— help, succour Is 30:5 (‖ הוֹעִיל, opp. בּשֶׁת, חֶרְפָּה), Dn 11:34; from י׳ ψ 20:3; 121:1, 2; 124:8; in 89:20 read; נֵזֶר for ע׳ Dy Gr Kau (עֹז Bi Che), cf. Hup; מָגֵן עֶזְר֑ךָ Dt 33:29) = י׳). concr. = one who helps (cf. i. עֶזְרָה 2) בְּע׳ (בְּ essent., v. ב I 7 b, Ges§ 119 h, i) Gn 2:18, 20 (J) + Ho 13:9 (read מִי בְע׳ Che We GuKau Now); coll. (without בְּ) Ez 12:14 (si vera l.; Co עֹזְרָיו); especially of י׳ ע׳ מִצָּרָיו Dt 33:7 (poem), ע׳ + מָגֵן ψ 33:20; 115:9, 10, 11, + מְפַלֵּט 70:6; בְּע׳ (v. supr.) Ex 18:4 (E), Dt 33:26 (poem), ψ 146:4. אֶבֶן הָעֵזֶר v. אֶבֶן (9).

### מֵעִם
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: מֵעִם  

from (lit. ‘away from being together with’).

**Etymology**: [Formed from the prep. מֵ◌ and עִם. cp. מֵאֵת.]

### מֵעִם

**Vocalized**: עַם  

v. sub I. עמם.

---

### מֵעִם
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עִם  

prep. with (Aramaic עִם, ܥܰܡ, Sab. עם DHMEpigr. Denkm. 12 HomChrest. 51; cf. Arabic مَعَ, مَعْ (but v. PrätBAS. i. 26)), sf. עִמִּי46 and עִמָּדִי45 (prob. akin to Arabic عِنْدٌ side, عِنْدَ beside: cf. √ ענד), without difference of meaning (עמי in Pent. Gn 31:31; 39:7, 12, 14; Ex 33:12 Lv 26:21, 23, 27, 40 [H] Nu 22:19; Jos-K. 13 times; ψ 42:9; 86:17; Jb 10:17; Ru 1:11; Est 7:8; in Dn 7 times, Ezr Ne Ch 11 times: עמדי in Pent. Gn [JE] 14 times, Ex 17:2; Lv 25:23; Dt 5:28; 32:34, 39; Ju S 7 times; ψ 23:4; 50:11; 50:19; 101:6; Jb 14 times, Ru 1:8); עִמְּךָ, †עִמְּכָה 1 S 1:26, עִמָּ֑ךְ; עִמּוֹ, עִמָּהּ; עִמָּ֫נוּ; עִמָּכֶם; עִמָּם27 (Gn 18:16, etc.: Gn-K 20 times; Ne 13:25; not Ezr Ch), and עִמָּהֶם23 (chiefly late: Nu 22:12; Dt 29:16 (v 24 עמם), Jon 1:3; Jb 1:4, and 19 times Ne Ezr Ch): of fellowship and companionship (Lat. cum), as Gn 13:1 ולוט עמו and Lot with him, 18:16; 19:30; 24:54 הוא והאנשׁים אשׁר עמו, וכל ישׂראל עמו Jos 7:24 al.; + often Peculiar to Ch Ezr are clauses introd. by ועמהם … 1 Ch 12:34; 13:2; 15:18 +, Ezr 8:13, 14 (Aramaic 5:2) +; so ועמו … 1 Ch 12:28; 2 Ch 17:14–18; 26:17; Ezr 8:3–12, 33. Coupling substantives, together with, chiefly in poetry: Dt 12:23 לא תאכל הנפשׁ עם הבשׂר, Jos 11:21; Ju 16:3; Ec 7:11; 2 S 1:24 שָׁנִי עִם עֲדָנִים, Dt 32:14 עִם חֵלֶב כרים, v 14, 24, 25 Am 4:10; Is 25:11; 34:7; Je 6:11; 51:40; Na 3:12 ψ 66:15 אעשׂה בקר עם עתודים, 81:3; 83:8; 87:4; 89:14; 104:25 +, Ct 1:11; 4:13(×2) כפרים עם נרדים, v 14(×2); 5:1(×3). Unusually, Est 9:25 אמר עם הספר he ordered in conjunction with writing, in a written order. Poet. or late uses: ψ 42:9 ובלילה שִׁירֹה עמי, ψ 89:22 אשׁר יָדִי תִּכּוֹן עמו, v 25; in Jb of the companionship of sufferings or prosperity, 6:4 חִצֵּי שַׁדַּי עמדי, 17:2 אם לא הֲתֻלִים עמדי, 29:6 וְצוּר יָצוּק עמדי פלגי שׁמן, v 20 כבודי חָדָשׁ עמדי, 2 Ch 14:5 ואין עמו מלחמה, 16:9 ישׁ עמך מלחמות, 28:10 עִמָּכֶם אֲשָׁמוֹת. Hence, in partic. of aid, Ex 23:5 Dt 22:4; 1 Ch 12:19; especially of God, Gn 21:22 אלהים עמך, 26:3 ואהיה עמך, v 33; 31:3, 5; Ex 3:12; Jos 1:5; Is 8:10 כ׳ עמנו אל ψ 23:4; 46:8, 12; Jb 29:5 + often (cf. אֵת, Gn 39:2, 3, 21, 23; Is 43:5 +): so הִתְחַזַּק עם 1 Ch 11:10; 2 Ch 16:9; Dn 10:21, עָזַר עם 1 Ch 12:21, כי גם ידם עם דוד 1 S 22:17; 2 S 3:12; 1 Ch 4:10 (cf. Ex 23:1, and II. אֵת 1 a) With the help of (= Gk. σύν), 1 S 14:45 כי עם אלהים עשׂה היום הזה, Dn 11:39 (rare). Of actions done jointly with another, as ירשׁ עם inherit with, Gn 21:10, כרת ברית עם 26:28 + often, עם (חִלֵּק) חָלַק to share with Jos 22:8; Is 53:12 +; cf. Dt 10:9 חֵלֶק וְנַחֲלָה עִם אֶחָיו, 18:1. If the common action be of the nature of a contest or combat, עִם is with in the sense of against: so often with נִלְחַם to fight, נִשְׁפַּט to dispute, נֶאֱבַק to wrestle, רִיב to strive (see these verbs), Gn 26:20; 30:8; 32:29; בּא בְּמִשְׁפָּט עם to enter into judgment with Is 3:14 +, Jb 14:3 (הֵבִיא), רִיב לי׳ עִם Hos 4:1 +; hence, without a verb expressing the idea explicitly, ψ 94:16 מי יקום לי עִם מְרֵעִים in the struggle with evil-doers? v b 55:19 כי בְרַבִּים היו עמדי for as many are they in combat with me, Jb 9:14 אָבְחֲרָה דְבָרַי עמו in the contest with him, 10:17b; 16:21; perhaps צָדַק עִם 9:2; 25:4 (cf. 4 b end). Of dealing with a person, or of the relation in which one stands with, or towards, another: as עשׂה חסד עם to do kindness with Gn 24:12 + often (in 1 pers. observe that in this phr. עמדי, not עמי, is regul. used, Gn 19:19; 20:13; 40:14; 1 S 20:14; 2 S 10:2 [‖ 1 Ch 19:2 עמי: here only], al.), עשׂה טוב עם Gn 26:29 +, v. also 20:9; 31:29; Dt 33:21; Ju 15:3; Jb 10:12; 13:20; 42:8 ψ 86:17 (cf. אִתְּכֶם Dt 1:30; 10:21; and Aramaic Dn 3:32), 119:124; 126:2 (1 S 12:24), Jb 33:29; הֵיטִיב עם Gn 32:10, 13 +, הֵרַע עִם 31:7; רָצָה עם be well pleased with ψ 50:18; Jb 34:9; דִּבֶּר עם to speak with (subj. usually God) Ex 19:9 + (v. דִּבֶּר 3 e), hence דָּבָר עִם Ju 18:7, 28; 2 S 3:17; 1 K 1:7; Jb 15:11 דָּבָר לָאַט עמך a word (spoken) gently with thee, cf. 11:5 ויפתח שׂפתיו עמך; יֵאָמֵן עם 2 Ch 1:9: and with adjj. תמים עם perfect with (i.e. toward) Dt 18:13 ψ 18:24, שָׁלֵם עם 1 K 8:61 +; cf. וְלִבָּם לֹא נָכוֹן עִמּוֹ ψ 78:37; 2 S 21:4; 23:5; 1 K 3:6 (עמך), Mi 6:8 הַצְנֵעַ לֶכֶת עִם אֱלֹהָיו, ψ 73:22 toward thee, 1 Ch 19:6; Hos 12:1 (?); also הָלַךְ קְרִי עִם Lv 26:21 ff. (7 times), מַמְרִים הֱיִיתֶם עם Dt 9:7, 24; 31:27; 2 Ch 16:10; 26:19 ψ 85:5; Jb 10:17 a. Of a common lot, Gn 18:23 האף תספה צדיק עם רשׁע together with the wicked, v 25 Jb 3:14, 15; Is 38:11 ψ 26:9 אל תאסף עם חטּאים נפשׁי, v b; 28:3(×2), 69:29; Jb 30:1; 1 Ch 24:5; hence like, ψ 73:5 ועם אדם לא יְנֻגָּ֑עוּ with (i.e. like) men (in general), 106:6 חטאנו עם אבותינו, Ec 2:16 ואיך ימות הֶחָכָם עם הכסיל i.e. like the fool. Of equality or resemblance generally (poet.), Jb 9:26 חָֽלְפוּ עִם אֳנִיּוֹת אֵבֶה they pass by with, i.e. as swiftly as, skiffs of reed, 37:18 תרקיע עמו שׁחקים i.e. like him, 40:15 אשׁר עשׂיתי עמך; so נִמְשַׁל עִם ψ 28:1; 143:7, נֶחְשַׁב עִם 88:5, cf. Ec 7:11. Of time, = as long as, ψ 72:5 יִירָאוּךָ עם שׁמשׁ as long as the sun endureth (cf. Aramaic Dn 3:33; 4:31; also Ovid. Am. i. 15, 16 cum sole et luna semper Aratus erit); at the time of (?) 2 Ch 21:19 (but read perhaps מֵחָלְיוֹ); and c. inf. (late), Ezr 1:11 עִם הֵעָלוֹת הַגּוֹלָה (cf. ἅμα τῷ sq. inf. Ju 3:21; 9:33; also עִם Ecclus 40:14). Of a locality, close to, beside: †Gn 25:11 וישׁב יצחק עם באר לחי ראי beside the well of, etc., 35:4 תַּחַת הָאֵלָה אשׁר עם שׁכם (so, after אשׁר, Jos 7:2; Ju 9:6; 18:22 2 S 13:23), Jos 19:46 (?), Ju 18:3 הֵמָּה עִם בֵּית מִיכָה (so 19:11; 2 S 20:8), 1 S 10:2; 2 S 6:7 וַיָּ֫מָת שָׁם עִם אֲרוֹן הא׳, 19:38; 24:16; 1 K 1:9; 1 Ch 13:14 by the house of O., in its own house (v. Be), 21:15 (2 S 24:16), 26:16 (cf. אֵת 2). By a person, Dt 5:28 וְאַתָּה עֲמֹד פֹּח עמדי by me, 1 S 1:26 הַנִּצֶּבֶת עִמְּכָה +; of one living near another, Ex 22:24; Lv 25:35, 36, 39, 47. Of persons, עִם is spec. in the house or family or service of (apud, chez): Gn 23:4 גֵּר וְתוֹשָׁב עמך (fig. of י׳ Lv 25:23 ψ 39:13), 27:44; 29:14 וַיֵּשֶׁב עִמּוֹ, v 25 עבדתי עמך I have served with thee (so v 30, cf. Dt 15:16), 31:38; Lv 25:6, 40(×2), 50, 53; Dt 22:2; 23:17; Ju 17:10; 2 S 19:34; 1 K 11:22; cf. 1 S 2:21 עם י׳ i.e. at his sanctuary. In the possession of (Lat. penes): Gn 24:25 straw, etc., is עִמָּנוּ, 31:32 Dt 17:19 והיתה עמו with him, at his side, 29:16; Jb 28:14 וְיָם אָמַר אֵין עִמָּדִי; = stored up with, Dt 32:34 כָּמֻם עִמָּדִי, Jb 27:13 (but read here prob. מֵאֵל; cf. 20:29); of ethical or mental attributes, 1 S 25:25 וּנְבָלָה עִמּוֹ, Jb 12:13 עִמּוֹ חָכְמָה וּגְבוּרָה, v 16; 25:2 ψ 130:7; 2 Ch 19:3, 7. cf. fig. ψ 36:10 כי עמך מְקוֹר חַיִּים. In the custody or care of, 1 S 9:23 שִׂים אֹתָהּ עִמָּ֑ךְ; 22:23 כִּי מִשְׁמֶרֶת אַתָּה עִמָּדִי: cf. Jb 17:3 עָרְבֵנִי עִמָּ֑ךְ be surety for me with thyself. Beside = except, Dt 32:39 אֵין אלהים עִמָּדִי, ψ 73:25 ועמך לא חפצתי בארץ and beside thee I delight not (in aught that is) upon earth (al. in comparison of thee, 1 f), 2 Ch 14:10 וְאֵין עִמְּךָ לַעֲזֹר there is none beside thee to help, 20:6; perhaps Hos 9:8 (Hi Ke; but very dub., v. Comm.). cf. אֵת 1 b. With = friendly with, Gn 31:2 כי איננו עמי (syn. v 5 אֶל towards), 2 K 10:15 לְבָבִי עִם לְבָֽבְךָ, Pr 23:7 וְלִבּוֹ בַל עִמָּ֑ךְ. Idiom. of a thought or purpose present with one:— עִם לְבַב פ׳, especially in Dt. and the later Deut. style, Dt 8:5 וְיָדַעְתָּ עִם לְבָבְךָ thou shalt know with thy heart, 15:9 פן יהיה דבר עם לבבך בליעל a wicked thought with thy heart, Jos 14:7 I brought back word כאשׁר עם לבבי; היה עם לְבַב פ׳, especially of a purpose, 1 K 8:17 ויהי עם לבב דוד לִבְנוֹת וגו׳ i.e. it was David’s purpose to build, etc., v 18(×2); 1 K 10:2 (= 2 Ch 6:7, 8(×2); 9:1), 1 Ch 22:7; 28:2 (both based on 1 K 8:17, etc.), 2 Ch 1:11; 24:4; 29:10; rather differently, to muse or talk with one’s heart, ψ 77:7; Ec 1:16. עִם alone, = in one’s consciousness, whether of knowledge or memory or purpose (cf. אֵת 3 b; عِنْدَ WAG ii. § 66): Nu 14:24 עֵקֶב הָֽיְתָה רוּחַ אַחֶרֶת עִמּוֹ i.e. operating in his mind, 1 K 11:11 יען אשׁר היתה זאת עמך, 1 Ch 28:12 the pattern of all אשׁר היה בָרוּחַ עמו that was in spirit (i.e. mentally) with him, ψ 50:11 וְזִיז שָׂדַי עִמָּדִי is with me (i.e. is known to me; ‖ ידעתי), prob. also 73:23; 139:18 (= in thy thought and care); Jb 9:35 לא כן אנכי עמדי not so am I with myself (my consciousness—or conscience—does not tell me that I need dread him), 10:13 ידעתי כי זאת עמך i.e. that this was thy purpose (‖ אֵלֶּה צָפַנְתָּ בִּלְבָבֶ֑ךָ), 15:9 ולא עמנו הוא and is not with us (= in our knowledge; ‖ וְלֹא נֵדָ֑ע), 23:10 כי ידע דרך עמדי the way of which I am conscious (De; Ew Di the way attending me, my usual way), v 14 וְכָהֵנָּה רַבּוֹת עמו i.e. he has many such purposes, 27:11 אֲשֶׁר עִם שַׁדָּי לֹא אֲכַחֵד i.e. his purposes or principles of action. Also sometimes in the judgment or estimation of, 1 S 2:26 טוֹב עִם, 2 S 6:22 נִכְבַּד עִם, perhaps צָדַק עִם Jb 9:2; 25:4 (Ges Bu; but Ew as 1 c, Hi De beside, in comparison of). Metaph. together with = in spite of, notwithstanding Ne 5:18 וְעִם זֶה (cf. مَعَ WAG ii. § 57; in Heb. בְּ is the more usual syn.).—In 1 S 16:12 אַדְמֹנִי עִם יְפֵה עֵינַיִם (cf. 17:42 עִם יְפֵה מַרְאֶה), if text correct, יפה must be a neuter subst., with beauty of eyes; but read prob. (Gr Bu) עֶלֶם a youth (17:56; 20:22) for עם (v. HPS; Che Expos. Times x. 521 אַדְמֹנִי עֶצֶם, cf. La 4:7).

---

### עֹשֵׂה
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עָשָׂה  

I. עָשָׂה vb. do, make (NH = BH; MI23.26 עשתי, l.3.9 ואעש, l.24 Imv. עשו; OH n.pr. עשיו, אלעשה, מעשיהו; not Aramaic, and not certainly found in S. Sem. lang.; on Sab. v. especially DHMSB Berl. Akkadian 1886, 849);— Pf. 3 ms. Gn 3:1 +, sf. עָשָׂהוּ Ho 8:6 +; 3 fs. עָֽשְׂתָה Dt 22:21 +, וְעָשָׂת consec. Lv 25:21; 2 ms. עָשִׂיתָ Gn 3:14 +, etc.; Impf. 3 ms. יַעֲשֶׂה Gn 18:25 +, וַיַּעֲשֶׂה 1 K 16:25 + 3 times, וַיַּעַשׂ Is 5:2 +; יַעֲשֵׂה Is 64:3, 2 ms. תַּעֲשֶׂה 1 S 10:8, תַּעֲשֵׂה Gn 26:29 Jos 7:9, juss. (c. אַל) תַּעַשׂ Gn 22:12 +, תַּעֲשֵׂה 2 S 13:12 Je 40:16 Qr (Kt תַּעַשׂ); 1 s. אֶעֱשֶׂה Gn 2:18 +, וָאֶעֱשֶׂה Ez 20:14 Dn 8:27, וָאַעַשׂ Dt 10:3 + 4 times Ez; etc.; Imv. ms. עֲשֵׂה Ju 10:15 +; fs. עֲשִׂי Je 6:26 +, etc.; Inf. abs. עָשׂה 44:17 +; עָשׂוֹ 7:5 + 2 times (4:18 van d. H. עָשׂוּ); cstr. עֲשׂוֹת Gn 2:4 +, עֲשֹׂה 50:20 + 2 times, עֲשׂוֹ 31:28, sf. עֲשׂתוֹ 41:32 +, עֲשׂהוּ †Ex 18:18, etc.; Pt. act. עֹשֶׂה Is 5:5 +, sf. הָעשֹׁוֹ Jb 40:19 (but v. infr.); pl. עֹשִׂים Gn 24:49 +; sf. עֹשָׂ֑י Jb 35:10 (yet v. Ges§ 124 k), etc.; pass. עָשׂוּי Ex 3:16 +, הֶעָשׂוּ Jb 41:25 (Ges§ 75 k); f. עֲשׂוּיָה Ez 21:20 +, etc.;— do (1560 + times): (1) c. acc. act. Gn 3:14 (J) 20:10 (E) + often; do right in eyes of י׳ 2 K 12:3; 14:13 +, evil 8:18, 27 + (cf. i. עַיִן 3 c); do God’s commands, statutes, etc., Dt 16:12; 30:8, Nu 15:39 (P); ע׳ חַיִל 1 S 14:48 + do might, i.e. mightily, valiantly; Pr 31:29 do efficiency, i.e. efficiently; = commit (offence) Lv 20:13 (H), Dt 20:18; sq. מִן part., וְעָשָׂה מֵאַחַת מֵהֵנָּה Lv 4:2 and shall do a single one of them (things prohibited; v. מִן 3 b (d)), cf. וְעָשׂוּ אַחַת מִכָּל־מִצְוֹת י׳ v 13, 22; 5:17 (all P); do = perform a work, c. acc. cogn. מַעֲשֶׂה Ex 18:20; 23:12 (E), also (י׳ subj.) ### שֹׁמֵר
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: שֹׁמֵר  

n.pr.m. father of one of Joash’s murderers 2 K 12:21, Σωμηρ, 𝔊L Σεμμηρ (called שִׁמְרִית q.v., a Moabitess, ‖ 2 Ch 24:26). Asherite 1 Ch 7:32, Σαμηρ, Σωμηρ; = שֶׁמֵר 2 b.

---

### צֵל
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צֵל  

shadow, shade. shelter, protection.

**Etymology**: [From צלל ᴵᴵᴵ. Related to Aram. טֽלָלָא, Syr. טֶלָּלָא, Ugar. ẓl, Ethiop. ṣelālōt, Akka. ṣillu (= shadow, shade), Aram. מַטְלַלְתָּא (= shelter, hut), Ugar. mẓll (= roofing), Arab. miẓalla, maẓalla (= umbrella, parasol, sunshade). cp. צִלָּה. and the second element in פְּלַגְצֵל. cp. also צַלֽמָוֶת.]  

**Derivatives**: Derivatives: צְלָלִי, צֽלָלִית.  

---

### צֵל
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צֵל  

v. III. צלל.

### צֵל
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צֵל²  

n.m. ψ 144:4 shadow;—צ׳ abs. 2 K 20:9 +, cstr. Gn 19:8 +; sf. צִלְּךָ ψ 121:5, צִלּוֹ Ez 31:6 +, also צִלְלוֹ Jb 40:22, etc.; pl. צְלָלִים Ct 2:17; 4:6; cstr. צִלְלֵי Je 6:4;— shadow on dial 2 K 20:9, 10(×2), 11 ‖ Is 38:8; צִלְלֵי־עָ֑רֶב Je 6:4, cf. Ct 2:17; 4:6; of mt. Ju 9:36. shadow, shade, as protection (from sun): of branches, Ju 9:15 (in allegory), Ho 4:13; Jb 40:22; Jon 4:5, 6 and (in fig.) Ho 14:8; Ez 17:23; 31:6, 12, 17 ψ 80:11 Ct 2:3 (of refreshment, delight); of rock Is 32:2 (sim.); צ׳ קֹרָתִי Gn 19:8 shadow of my roof; of cloud Is 25:4 and (fig. of י׳’s protection 4:6; 25:4, so prob. 16:3; צ׳ כְּנָפֶיךָ shadow of thy wings, fig. of י׳’s protection ψ 17:8; 36:8; 57:2; 63:8; shade, in gen., Jb 7:2, fig. of י׳ ψ 121:5; hence = protection, defence, of city (wall) Je 48:45; of י׳ Nu 14:9 (JE), ψ 91:1 (v. also n.pr. בְּצַלְאֵל), cf. צֵל יָדוֹ Is 49:2 (covering prophet, under fig. of sword), 51:16 (covering people); of human ally Is 30:2, 3, of king La 4:20; also of wisdom Ec 7:12, money v 12.—בְּצִלָּהּ Is 34:15 appar. ref. to קִפּוֹז, but improb.; read perhaps בֵּצֶיהָ her eggs Du CheHpt. (CheHeb. Hpt. transp. וּבָֽקְעָה וְדָֽגְרָה). shadow as symb. of transitoriness of life Jb 8:9; 14:2 ψ 144:4 (כְּצֵל עוֹבֵר), Ec 6:12; 8:13; כְּצֵל נָטוּי of an individ. life near its end ψ 102:12, so כְּצֵל כִּנְטוֹתוֹ 109:23; in sim. of emaciated members, וִיצֻרַי כַּצֵּל Jb 17:7.

Smite fatally: (subj. man) smite, c. acc., + word of killing (dying):—obj. lion and bear 1 S 17:35; man Ex 21:12, 20 (E), Jos 10:26 (JE), 11:17 (D), 1 S 17:50; 2 S 1:15; 2:31 (מִן partit. + ב among), 4:7; 14:6; 18:15; 21:17; 1 K 16:10; 2 K 12:22; 15:30; 25:21 = Je 52:27, 2 K 25:25 Je 41:2; Nu 35:16, 17, 18, 21 (P; all c. ב instr.); c. acc. + אֶל־חֹמֶשׁ + ב instr. 2 S 2:23 smote him (fatally) in the belly with (v. HPS), 20:10, cf. 3:27 (+ 2 acc.) 4:6 (but del. 𝔊 Ew Th We Dr Bu HPS); וְהִכָּהוּ נֶפֶשׁ Dt 19:11 and he smite him in his life (mortally), and he die, cf. (without word of dying) v 6 Gn 37:21 (J), Je 40:14, 15 [otherwise Lv 24:17.18 Nu 35:11, 15, 30; Dt 27:25; Jos 20:3, 9]; with prolepsis לְהַכּוֹת חֲלָלִים smite the slain Ju 20:31, 39; + אַ֫רְצָה smite to the ground i.e. kill 2 S 2:22; 18:11. smite, of worm gnawing or boring so as to kill plant Jon 4:7. very often = kill, slay, man or beast (c. 150 times): Gn 4:15; 8:21 (exterminate; both J), Ex 2:12; Nu 21:35 (E), Jos 7:5 (מִן partit.), v 5 (JE), Dt 19:6; 21:1; 27:24, 25; Jos 11:10 (ב instr.; all D), Lv 24:17, 18, 21(×2) (H), Nu 35:11, 15, 21, 24, 30; Jos 9:18; 20:3, 9 (all P), Ju 15:16; 1 S 17:9(×2), 36; 2 S 12:9 (ב instr.), 2 K 9:7 (exterminate), etc.; c. ב among 1 S 6:19; 23:5, מִן partit. Ju 14:19; 20:45; c. ב partit. slay at, work slaughter among 2 S 23:10 (ins. also in ‖ 1 Ch 11:13 v Bead loc. Dr 2 S 23:10), but also ב of dir. obj. 1 S 18:7; 21:12 = 29:5; subj. lion 1 K 20:36(×2) cf. Je 5:6 (fig. of judgment); י׳ subj. 1 S 6:19b (ב among, + acc. cogn.; 1 S 6:19a וַיַּךְ crpt. v. 𝔊 Th We Dr Kl Kit Bu HPS); slay firstborn Ex 12:12 (J), v 29 Nu 3:13; 8:17; 33:4 (all P), ψ 78:51; 105:36; 135:8; God slays for (עַל) sin 2 S 6:7 = 1 Ch 13:10 (עַל־אֲשֶׁר); slay לְפִי־חֶרֶב Dt 13:16; 20:13 Jos 11:11, 12, 14 (all D), Ju 18:27; 21:10; 1 S 22:9; 2 K 10:25 Je 21:7 Jb 1:15, 17; kill, slay, c. acc. cogn. (מַכָּה) 1 S 14:14; 1 K 20:21 (ב among), 2 Ch 13:17 (בָּחֶם), 25:13 (מֵהֶם); c. acc. pers. + acc. cogn. Jos 10:20 (JE), Est 9:5, etc.; kill unwittingly, unintentionally הִכָּה בִבְלִי דַ֫עַת Dt 19:4; Jos 20:5 (both D). Smite = attack, attack and destroy a company Gn 32:9, 12; 34:30; Jos 8:21 Ju 8:11; 9:43; 2 K 8:21; 2 Ch 21:9; attack and capture a city Ju 1:8 v 12 = Jos 15:16 (JE), Jos 7:3; 10:4 (JE), 1 S 30:1; 1 K 15:20 = 2 Ch 16:4, 2 K 15:16(×2) 1 Ch 20:1; 2 Ch 14:13; Je 47:1; tents 1 Ch 4:41; 2 Ch 14:14; = sack a city לְפִי־חֶרֶב (slaying inhab.), Jos 8:24; 19:47 (both JE), 10:28, 30, 32, 35, 37 cf. v 39 (all D), Ju 1:25; 20:37; 2 S 15:14; especially defeat kg. or army (involving often overthrow, pursuit and slaughter),—c. 95 times,—Gn 14:5, 15, 17; Nu 14:45; 21:24 (JE), Jos 8:12; 10:10 (+ acc. cogn.), v 10 (עַד loc.), 11:8(×2) (all JE), Dt 1:4; 2:33; 3:3; 4:46; 7:2; 29:6; Jos 10:33, 40 (ועד—מִן loc.), 12:1, 6, 12; 13:12 (all D); Ju 1:5, 17; 1 S 11:11 (עד temp.), 2 K 13:17 (עַד־כְּלֵּה), 2 K 3:24a + v 24b (where read וַיָּבֹאוּ בוֹא וְהַכּוֹת and they went on defeating), Is 10:20; Je 37:10, etc.; ins. וַיַּךְ or הִכָּה in 2 S 8:13 Th Ke Kit Bu cf. Dr, after 𝔊; c. ב obj. 1 S 14:31; 23:2(×2) (ה loc.—מִן); + לְפִי חֶרֶב Ju 20:48, etc.; of gods causing defeat 2 Ch 28:23; smite land = conquer, subjugate, sometimes ravage, Gn 14:7; Jos 10:40 (D), 1 S 27:9; Je 43:11; 46:13, cf. Is 14:6 (acc. cogn.); subj. י׳ Nu 32:4 (P); of י׳ smiting sea וְהִכָּה בַיָּם גַּלִּים Zc 10:11 (BevJPh. xviii. 35 (1889), 88 proposes גבלים). Of God, smite with (ב) a plague, disease, etc.:—blindness Gn 19:11 (J), 2 K 6:18(×2) cf. Zc 12:4(×2) (symbol.); of Egyptian plagues Ex 3:20; 9:15 (both J), 1 S 4:8, without ב Ex 7:25 (J), 12:13 (P), ψ 136:10 (acc. + בִּבְכוֹרֵיהֶם); other plagues Nu 14:12 (JE), Dt 28:22, 27, 28, 35; 1 S 5:6; 2 S 24:17; Jb 2:7; Am 4:9; Hg 2:17; Mal 3:24; 1 S 5:6, 9; 2 K 19:35 = Is 37:36 (+ ב in); Nu 11:33 (JE; בָּעָם + acc. cogn.); smite vines with (ב) blight ψ 105:33. smite = chastise, or send judgment upon, usually c. acc., 1 K 14:15; 1 Ch 21:7; Is 5:25; 9:12; 27:7 (הַכְּמַכַּת מַכֵּהוּ הִכָּהוּ), 30:31 (ב instr.) 57:17; 60:10 (opp. רִחַם) Je 2:30; 5:3; 14:9; Ez 32:15 (𝔊 Co זֵרָה scatter); c. עַל punish for, (sin) Lv 26:24 (H). of God’s destroying palaces Am 3:15; 6:11 (2 acc.), cf. Zc 9:4. Pf. הֻכָּה Ho 9:16; Nu 25:14. הוּכָּה ψ 102:5; וְהֻכָּת consec. Ex 22:1; 3 fs. הֻכְּתָה Ez 33:21; 40:1; 1 s. הֻכֵּ֫יתִי Zc 13:6; 3 pl. הֻכּוּ 1 S 5:12; Impf. 3 mpl. וַיּכֻּוּ Ex 5:14; 2 mpl. תֻּכּוּ Is 1:5; Pt. מֻכֶּה Nu 25:14, cstr. מֻכֵּה Is 53:4; f. מֻכָּה Nu 25:15, 18; pl. מֻפִּים Ex 5:16, cstr. מֻכֵּי־ Je 18:21;—be smitten: = receive a blow Is 1:5 (Judah under fig. of man). be wounded Zc 13:6. be beaten Ex 5:14, 16 (J). be (fatally) smitten + vb. of dying Ex 22:1 (E); be killed, slain Nu 25:14(×2), 15, 18 (c. עַל, for), מֻכֵּי־חֶרֶב Je 18:21 (‖ הֲרֻגֵי מָ֑וֶת); so also (abs.) prob. 1 S 4:2 (read וַיֻּכּוּ 𝔊 𝔖 𝔙 Th Dr Klo Kit, for MT וַיַּכּוּ). be attacked and captured, of city Ez 33:21; 40:1. be smitten with disease (by God) 1 S 5:12; abs., of י׳’s servant Is 53:4. be blighted, of plant (in fig.) Ho 9:16 (Ephr.), ψ 102:5 (heart, כָּעֵשֶׂב; both + יָבַשׁ).

### יָרֵחַ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: יָרֵחַ  

n.m. Jos 10:13 moon, especially poet. (NH id.; Ph. ירח; Assyrian iriḥu, according to PinchesBOR Aug. 1888, 207; Ethiopic ወርሕ moon, month; cf. sub יֶרַח)—abs. יָרֵחַ Gn 37:9 + 25 times; sf. וִיֽרֵחֵךְ Is 60:20:—moon, usually named with sun Jos 10:12, 13 (poem in JE), ψ 72:5 (עִם־שָׁ֑מֶשׁ וְלִפְנֵי יָרֵחַ), 89:38 (in these two, a symbol of permanence), 121:6 Is 60:19 Hb 3:11 Jo 3:4; obj. of idolatrous worship Jb 31:26 (+ sun); in same sense also + sun and stars Dt 4:19; 17:3 2 K 23:5 Je 8:2; as determiner of feast-times ψ 104:19 (‖ sun); + stars, as shining by night ψ 136:9 (‖ sun, by day), so חֻקֹּת יָרֵחַ Je 31:35 (‖ id.); elsewhere + sun and stars Gn 37:9 (E), Is 13:10 Ez 32:7 Jo 2:10; 4:15 ψ 148:3 Ec 12:2; + stars ψ 8:4 Jb 25:5; with neither sun nor stars only עַד־בְּלִי יָרֵחַ ψ 72:7.
